style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;display:block;margin-top:0.4em; line-height:1.6em;padding-bottom:0.5em;">\n<ul><li><img alt="Category" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" title="Category" width="16" height="14" href="/wiki/Category:Physical_cosmology" title="Category:Physical cosmology">Category</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/File:Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png" class="image"><img alt="Ilc 9yr moll4096.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/16px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png" width="16" height="8" class="noviewer" data-file-width="3864" data-file-height="3864" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Astronomy" title="Portal:Astronomy">Astronomy&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Physical_cosmology" title="Template talk:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Sir Isaac Newton</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Royal_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="President of the Royal Society">PRS</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span> (25 December 1642&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 20 March <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">1726/27</a><sup id="cite_ref-OSNS_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSNS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>) was an English <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theologian" class="mw-redirect" title="Theologian">theologian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Author" title="Author">author</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physicist</a> (described in his own day as a "<a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosopher</a>") who is widely recognised as one of the most influential scientists of all time, and a key figure in the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>.  His book <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a></i> ("Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy"), first published in 1687, laid the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>.  Newton also made seminal contributions to <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, and shares credit with <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz</a> for developing the infinitesimal <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>.\n</p><p>In <i>Principia</i>, Newton formulated the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">laws of motion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">universal gravitation</a> that formed the dominant scientific viewpoint until being superseded by the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">theory of relativity</a>. Newton used his mathematical description of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> to prove <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler&#39;s laws of planetary motion">Kepler\'s laws of planetary motion</a>, account for the <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectories</a> of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Axial_precession" title="Axial precession">precession of the equinoxes</a>, and other phenomena, eradicating doubt about the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentricity</a>. He demonstrated that the <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)" title="Dynamics (mechanics)">motion of objects</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">celestial bodies</a> could be accounted for by the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">same principles</a>. Newton\'s prediction that the Earth is an <a href="/wiki/Spheroid#Oblate_spheroids" title="Spheroid">oblate spheroid</a> was later vindicated by the geodetic measurements of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Louis_Maupertuis" title="Pierre Louis Maupertuis">Maupertuis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Marie_de_La_Condamine" title="Charles Marie de La Condamine">La Condamine</a>, and others, convincing most European scientists of the superiority of Newtonian mechanics over earlier systems.\n</p><p>Newton also built the first practical <a href="/wiki/Reflecting_telescope" title="Reflecting telescope">reflecting telescope</a> and developed a sophisticated theory of colour based on the observation that a <a href="/wiki/Dispersive_prism" title="Dispersive prism">prism</a> separates <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum#Visible_radiation_(light)" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">white light</a> into the colours of the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>. His work on light was collected in his highly influential book <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i>, published in 1704. He also formulated an <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_cooling" title="Newton&#39;s law of cooling">empirical law of cooling</a>, made the first theoretical calculation of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>, and introduced the notion of a <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_fluid" title="Newtonian fluid">Newtonian fluid</a>.  In addition to his work on calculus, as a mathematician Newton contributed to the study of power series, generalised the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_theorem" title="Binomial theorem">binomial theorem</a> to non-integer exponents, developed a <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_method" title="Newton&#39;s method">method</a> for approximating the <a href="/wiki/Zero_of_a_function" title="Zero of a function">roots of a function</a>, and classified most of the <a href="/wiki/Cubic_plane_curve" title="Cubic plane curve">cubic plane curves</a>.\n</p><p>Newton was a fellow of <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College</a> and the second <a href="/wiki/Lucasian_Professor_of_Mathematics" title="Lucasian Professor of Mathematics">Lucasian Professor of Mathematics</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>.  He was a devout but unorthodox Christian, who privately rejected the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> and who, unusually for a member of the Cambridge faculty of the day, refused to take <a href="/wiki/Holy_orders" title="Holy orders">holy orders</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.  Beyond his work on the mathematical sciences, Newton dedicated much of his time to the study of <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_Bible" title="Chronology of the Bible">biblical chronology</a>, but most of his work in those areas remained unpublished until long after his death.  Politically and personally tied to the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig party</a>, Newton served two brief terms as Member of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a> for the University of Cambridge, in 1689\xe2\x80\x9390 and 1701\xe2\x80\x9302.  He was <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knighted</a> by <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a> in 1705 and he spent the last three decades of his life in London, serving as <a href="/wiki/Warden_of_the_Mint" title="Warden of the Mint">Warden</a> (1696\xe2\x80\x931700) and <a href="/wiki/Master_of_the_Mint" title="Master of the Mint">Master</a> (1700\xe2\x80\x931727) of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">Royal Mint</a>, as well as president of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> (1703\xe2\x80\x931727).\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Life"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Early_life"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Middle_years"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Middle years</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-4"><a href="#Mathematics"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Mathematics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-5"><a href="#Optics"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Optics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-6"><a href="#Mechanics_and_gravitation"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Mechanics and gravitation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-7"><a href="#Classification_of_cubics"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Classification of cubics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Later_life"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Later life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Personal_relations"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Personal relations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#After_death"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">After death</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Fame"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Fame</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Commemorations"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Commemorations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Religious_views"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Religious views</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Effect_on_religious_thought"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Effect on religious thought</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Occult"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Occult</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Alchemy"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Alchemy</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Enlightenment_philosophers"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Enlightenment philosophers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Apple_incident"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Apple incident</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Works"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Works</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Published_in_his_lifetime"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Published in his lifetime</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Published_posthumously"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Published posthumously</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed" style="float:right; width:300px">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>A timeline of Newton\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n<p>1642: Born on 25 December\n</p><p>1661: Admitted at Trinity College, Cambridge.\n</p><p>1665: Discovered the generalised <a href="/wiki/Binomial_theorem" title="Binomial theorem">binomial theorem</a>; Began to develop <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>\n</p><p>1667: Returned to Cambridge; elected as a fellow of Trinity\n</p><p>1687: Publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a></i>\n</p><p>1704: Publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i>\n</p><p>1727: Death of Newton, in London. \n</p><p>1728: Publication of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronology_of_Ancient_Kingdoms_Amended" title="The Chronology of Ancient Kingdoms Amended">The Chronology of Ancient Kingdoms Amended</a></i>\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Isaac_Newton" title="Early life of Isaac Newton">Early life of Isaac Newton</a></div>\n<p>Isaac Newton was born (according to the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>, in use in England at the time) on Christmas Day, 25 December 1642 (<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">NS</a> 4 January 1643<sup id="cite_ref-OSNS_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSNS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>) "an hour or two after midnight",<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Woolsthorpe_Manor" title="Woolsthorpe Manor">Woolsthorpe Manor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Woolsthorpe-by-Colsterworth" title="Woolsthorpe-by-Colsterworth">Woolsthorpe-by-Colsterworth</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hamlet_(place)" title="Hamlet (place)">hamlet</a> in the county of Lincolnshire. His father, also named Isaac Newton, had died three months before. <a href="/wiki/Premature_birth" class="mw-redirect" title="Premature birth">Born prematurely</a>, Newton was a small child; his mother Hannah Ayscough reportedly said that he could have fit inside a <a href="/wiki/Quart" title="Quart">quart</a> mug.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> When Newton was three, his mother remarried and went to live with her new husband, the Reverend Barnabas Smith, leaving her son in the care of his maternal grandmother, Margery Ayscough. The young Isaac disliked his stepfather and maintained some enmity towards his mother for marrying him, as revealed by this entry in a list of sins committed up to the age of 19: "Threatening my father and mother Smith to burn them and the house over them."<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Newton\'s mother had three children from her second marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall198055_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall198055-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the age of about twelve until he was seventeen, Newton was educated at <a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_School,_Grantham" title="The King&#39;s School, Grantham">The King\'s School, Grantham</a>, which taught Latin and Greek and probably imparted a significant foundation of mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He was removed from school, and returned to Woolsthorpe-by-Colsterworth by October 1659. His mother, widowed for the second time, attempted to make him a farmer, an occupation he hated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall199416\xe2\x80\x9319_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall199416\xe2\x80\x9319-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Henry Stokes, master at the King\'s School, persuaded his mother to send him back to school. Motivated partly by a desire for revenge against a schoolyard bully, he became the top-ranked student,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite199722_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite199722-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> distinguishing himself mainly by building <a href="/wiki/Sundial" title="Sundial">sundials</a> and models of windmills.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall198060\xe2\x80\x9362_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall198060\xe2\x80\x9362-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 1661, he was admitted to <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a>, on the recommendation of his uncle Rev William Ayscough, who had studied there. He started as a <a href="/wiki/Subsizar" class="mw-redirect" title="Subsizar">subsizar</a>\xe2\x80\x94paying his way by performing <a href="/wiki/Valet" title="Valet">valet</a>\'s duties\xe2\x80\x94until he was awarded a scholarship in 1664, guaranteeing him four more years until he could get his MA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall198071,103_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall198071,103-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> At that time, the college\'s teachings were based on those of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, whom Newton supplemented with modern philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes">Descartes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astronomers" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomers">astronomers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Street" title="Thomas Street">Thomas Street</a>, through whom he learned of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a>\'s work. He set down in his notebook a series of "<a href="/wiki/Quaestiones_quaedam_philosophicae" title="Quaestiones quaedam philosophicae"><i>Quaestiones</i></a>" about <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy" title="Mechanical philosophy">mechanical philosophy</a> as he found it. In 1665, he discovered the generalised <a href="/wiki/Binomial_theorem" title="Binomial theorem">binomial theorem</a> and began to develop a mathematical theory that later became <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>. Soon after Newton had obtained his BA degree in August 1665, the university temporarily closed as a precaution against the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plague_of_London" title="Great Plague of London">Great Plague</a>. Although he had been undistinguished as a Cambridge student,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Newton\'s private studies at his home in Woolsthorpe over the subsequent two years saw the development of his theories on <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of gravitation">law of gravitation</a>.\n</p><p>In April 1667, he returned to Cambridge and in October was elected as a fellow of Trinity.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980178_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980178-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Fellows were required to become ordained priests, although this was not enforced in the restoration years and an assertion of conformity to the Church of England was sufficient. However, by 1675 the issue could not be avoided and by then his unconventional views stood in the way.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980330\xe2\x80\x931_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980330\xe2\x80\x931-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Newton managed to avoid it by means of a special permission from <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> (see "Middle years" section below).\n</p><p>His studies had impressed the <a href="/wiki/Lucasian_Professor_of_Mathematics" title="Lucasian Professor of Mathematics">Lucasian professor</a> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Barrow" title="Isaac Barrow">Isaac Barrow</a>, who was more anxious to develop his own religious and administrative potential (he became master of Trinity two years later); in 1669 Newton succeeded him, only one year after receiving his MA. He was elected a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1672" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1672">Fellow of the Royal Society (FRS) in 1672</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frs_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_years">Middle years</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolton-newton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bolton-newton.jpg/220px-Bolton-newton.jpg" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bolton-newton.jpg/330px-Bolton-newton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bolton-newton.jpg/440px-Bolton-newton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1338" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolton-newton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isaac Newton (<i>Bolton, Sarah K. Famous Men of Science. NY: Thomas Y. Crowell &amp; Co., 1889</i>)</div></div></div><p>\nNewton\'s work has been said "to distinctly advance every branch of mathematics then studied".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1908319_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1908319-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> His work on the subject usually referred to as fluxions or calculus, seen in a manuscript of October 1666, is now published among Newton\'s mathematical papers.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The author of the manuscript <i><a href="/wiki/De_analysi_per_aequationes_numero_terminorum_infinitas" title="De analysi per aequationes numero terminorum infinitas">De analysi per aequationes numero terminorum infinitas</a></i>, sent by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Barrow" title="Isaac Barrow">Isaac Barrow</a> to <a href="/wiki/John_Collins_(mathematician)" title="John Collins (mathematician)">John Collins</a> in June 1669, was identified by Barrow in a letter sent to Collins in August of that year as:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGjertsen1986149_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGjertsen1986149-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  </p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Mr Newton, a fellow of our College, and very young&#160;... but of an extraordinary genius and proficiency in these things. \n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Newton later became involved in a dispute with <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a> over priority in the development of calculus (the <a href="/wiki/Leibniz%E2%80%93Newton_calculus_controversy" title="Leibniz\xe2\x80\x93Newton calculus controversy">Leibniz\xe2\x80\x93Newton calculus controversy</a>). Most modern historians believe that Newton and Leibniz developed <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> independently, although with very different <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_notation" title="Mathematical notation">notations</a>. Occasionally it has been suggested that Newton published almost nothing about it until 1693, and did not give a full account until 1704, while Leibniz began publishing a full account of his methods in 1684. (Leibniz\'s notation and "differential Method", nowadays recognised as much more convenient notations, were adopted by continental European mathematicians, and after 1820 or so, also by British mathematicians.) But such a suggestion fails to account for the content of calculus in <a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica#Book_1,_De_motu_corporum" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Book 1</a> of Newton\'s <i>Principia</i> itself (published 1687) and in its forerunner manuscripts, such as <i><a href="/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum" title="De motu corporum in gyrum">De motu corporum in gyrum</a></i> ("On the motion of bodies in orbit") of 1684; this content has been pointed out by critics of both Newton\'s time and modern times. The <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i> is not written in the language of calculus either as we know it or as Newton\'s (later) \'dot\' notation would write it. His work extensively uses calculus in geometric form based on limiting values of the ratios of vanishing small quantities: in the <i>Principia</i> itself, Newton gave demonstration of this under the name of \'the method of first and last ratios\'<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and explained why he put his expositions in this form,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> remarking also that \'hereby the same thing is performed as by the method of indivisibles\'.\n</p><p>Because of this, the <i>Principia</i> has been called "a book dense with the theory and application of the infinitesimal calculus" in modern times<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and "lequel est presque tout de ce calcul" (\'nearly all of it is of this calculus\') in Newton\'s time.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> His use of methods involving "one or more orders of the infinitesimally small" is present in his <i>De motu corporum in gyrum</i> of 1684<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and in his papers on motion "during the two decades preceding 1684".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/220px-Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/330px-Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/440px-Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newton in a 1702 portrait by <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller" title="Godfrey Kneller">Godfrey Kneller</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Newton had been reluctant to publish his calculus because he feared controversy and criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart2009107_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart2009107-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> He was close to the Swiss mathematician <a href="#Duillier">Nicolas Fatio de Duillier</a>. In 1691, Duillier started to write a new version of Newton\'s <i>Principia</i>, and corresponded with Leibniz.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980538\xe2\x80\x93539_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980538\xe2\x80\x93539-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In 1693, the relationship between Duillier and Newton deteriorated and the book was never completed.\n</p><p>Starting in 1699, other members of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> (of which Newton was a member) accused Leibniz of <a href="/wiki/Plagiarism" title="Plagiarism">plagiarism</a>. The dispute then broke out in full force in 1711 when the Royal Society proclaimed in a study that it was Newton who was the true discoverer and labelled Leibniz a fraud. This study was cast into doubt when it was later found that Newton himself wrote the study\'s concluding remarks on Leibniz. Thus began the bitter controversy which marred the lives of both Newton and Leibniz until the latter\'s death in 1716.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1908356ff_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1908356ff-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newton is generally credited with the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_theorem#Newton&#39;s_generalised_binomial_theorem" title="Binomial theorem">generalised binomial theorem</a>, valid for any exponent. He discovered <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_identities" title="Newton&#39;s identities">Newton\'s identities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_method" title="Newton&#39;s method">Newton\'s method</a>, classified <a href="/wiki/Cubic_plane_curve" title="Cubic plane curve">cubic plane curves</a> (<a href="/wiki/Polynomials" class="mw-redirect" title="Polynomials">polynomials</a> of degree three in two <a href="/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)" title="Variable (mathematics)">variables</a>), made substantial contributions to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Finite_differences" class="mw-redirect" title="Finite differences">finite differences</a>, and was the first to use fractional indices and to employ <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_geometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Coordinate geometry">coordinate geometry</a> to derive solutions to <a href="/wiki/Diophantine_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Diophantine equations">Diophantine equations</a>. He approximated <a href="/wiki/Series_(mathematics)" title="Series (mathematics)">partial</a> sums of the <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_series_(mathematics)" title="Harmonic series (mathematics)">harmonic series</a> by <a href="/wiki/Logarithms" class="mw-redirect" title="Logarithms">logarithms</a> (a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_summation_formula" class="mw-redirect" title="Euler&#39;s summation formula">Euler\'s summation formula</a>) and was the first to use <a href="/wiki/Power_series" title="Power series">power series</a> with confidence and to revert power series. Newton\'s work on infinite series was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a>\'s decimals.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Newton received his MA and became a Fellow of the "College of the Holy and Undivided Trinity" in 1667, he made the commitment that "I will either set Theology as the object of my studies and will take holy orders when the time prescribed by these statutes [7 years] arrives, or I will resign from the college."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980179_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980179-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Up till this point he had not thought much about religion and had twice signed his agreement to the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-nine_articles" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty-nine articles">thirty-nine articles</a>, the basis of <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> doctrine.\n</p><p>He was appointed <a href="/wiki/Lucasian_Professor_of_Mathematics" title="Lucasian Professor of Mathematics">Lucasian Professor of Mathematics</a> in 1669 on Barrow\'s recommendation. During that time, any Fellow of a college at Cambridge or Oxford was required to take <a href="/wiki/Holy_orders" title="Holy orders">holy orders</a> and become an ordained <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglican</a> priest. However, the terms of the Lucasian professorship required that the holder <i>not</i> be active in the church (presumably so as to have more time for science). Newton argued that this should exempt him from the ordination requirement, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a>, whose permission was needed, accepted this argument. Thus a conflict between Newton\'s religious views and Anglican orthodoxy was averted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1997151_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1997151-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Optics">Optics</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg/220px-NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg/330px-NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg/440px-NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="891" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewtonsTelescopeReplica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of Newton\'s second <a href="/wiki/Reflecting_telescope" title="Reflecting telescope">reflecting telescope</a>, which he presented to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> in 1672<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1666, Newton observed that the spectrum of colours exiting a <a href="/wiki/Triangular_prism_(optics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Triangular prism (optics)">prism</a> in the position of <a href="/wiki/Minimum_deviation" title="Minimum deviation">minimum deviation</a> is oblong, even when the light ray entering the prism is circular, which is to say, the prism refracts different colours by different angles.<sup id="cite_ref-Whittaker_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whittaker-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This led him to conclude that colour is a property intrinsic to light\xe2\x80\x94a point which had been debated in prior years.\n</p><p>From 1670 to 1672, Newton lectured on optics.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> During this period he investigated the <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> of light, demonstrating that the multicoloured spectrum produced by a prism could be recomposed into white light by a <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lens</a> and a second prism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1908324_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1908324-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Modern scholarship has revealed that Newton\'s analysis and resynthesis of white light owes a debt to <a href="/wiki/Corpuscularianism" title="Corpuscularianism">corpuscular</a> alchemy.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He showed that coloured light does not change its properties by separating out a coloured beam and shining it on various objects, and that regardless of whether reflected, scattered, or transmitted, the light remains the same colour. Thus, he observed that colour is the result of objects interacting with already-coloured light rather than objects generating the colour themselves. This is known as <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Isaac_Newton#Newton&#39;s_theory_of_colour" title="Early life of Isaac Newton">Newton\'s theory of colour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall1908325_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall1908325-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg/220px-Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg/330px-Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg/440px-Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dispersive_Prism_Illustration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of a <a href="/wiki/Dispersive_prism" title="Dispersive prism">dispersive prism</a> separating white light into the colours of the spectrum, as discovered by Newton</div></div></div>\n<p>From this work, he concluded that the lens of any <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">refracting telescope</a> would suffer from the <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">dispersion</a> of light into colours (<a href="/wiki/Chromatic_aberration" title="Chromatic aberration">chromatic aberration</a>). As a proof of the concept, he constructed a telescope using reflective mirrors instead of lenses as the <a href="/wiki/Objective_(optics)" title="Objective (optics)">objective</a> to bypass that problem.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Early_Period_(1608\xe2\x80\x931672)_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Early_Period_(1608\xe2\x80\x931672)-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-White_1997,_p170_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White_1997,_p170-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Building the design, the first known functional reflecting telescope, today known as a <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_telescope" title="Newtonian telescope">Newtonian telescope</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-White_1997,_p170_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White_1997,_p170-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> involved solving the problem of a suitable mirror material and shaping technique. Newton ground his own mirrors out of a custom composition of highly reflective <a href="/wiki/Speculum_metal" title="Speculum metal">speculum metal</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_rings" title="Newton&#39;s rings">Newton\'s rings</a> to judge the <a href="/wiki/Quality_(philosophy)" title="Quality (philosophy)">quality</a> of the optics for his telescopes. In late 1668<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> he was able to produce this first reflecting telescope. It was about eight inches long and it gave a clearer and larger image. In 1671, the Royal Society asked for a demonstration of his reflecting telescope.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1997168_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1997168-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Their interest encouraged him to publish his notes, <i>Of Colours</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> which he later expanded into the work <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i>. When <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> criticised some of Newton\'s ideas, Newton was so offended that he withdrew from public debate. Newton and Hooke had brief exchanges in 1679\xe2\x80\x9380, when Hooke, appointed to manage the Royal Society\'s correspondence, opened up a correspondence intended to elicit contributions from Newton to Royal Society transactions,<sup id="cite_ref-hooke1679nov24_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hooke1679nov24-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> which had the effect of stimulating Newton to work out a proof that the elliptical form of planetary orbits would result from a centripetal force inversely proportional to the square of the radius vector (see <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation#History" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">Newton\'s law of universal gravitation&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 History</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum" title="De motu corporum in gyrum">De motu corporum in gyrum</a></i>). But the two men remained generally on poor terms until Hooke\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg/170px-Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg/255px-Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg/340px-Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2439" data-file-height="3377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newton-letter-to-briggs_03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Facsimile of a 1682 letter from Isaac Newton to Dr William Briggs, commenting on Briggs\' "A New Theory of Vision"</div></div></div>\n<p>Newton argued that light is composed of particles or corpuscles, which were refracted by accelerating into a denser medium. He verged on soundlike waves to explain the repeated pattern of reflection and transmission by thin films (Opticks Bk.II, Props. 12), but still retained his theory of \'fits\' that disposed corpuscles to be reflected or transmitted (Props.13). However, later physicists favoured a purely wavelike explanation of light to account for the <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> patterns and the general phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a>. Today\'s <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photons" class="mw-redirect" title="Photons">photons</a>, and the idea of <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave\xe2\x80\x93particle duality">wave\xe2\x80\x93particle duality</a> bear only a minor resemblance to Newton\'s understanding of light.\n</p><p>In his <i>Hypothesis of Light</i> of 1675, Newton <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/posit" class="extiw" title="wikt:posit">posited</a> the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">ether</a> to transmit forces between particles. The contact with the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Platonists" title="Cambridge Platonists">Cambridge Platonist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Henry_More" title="Henry More">Henry More</a> revived his interest in alchemy.<sup id="cite_ref-More_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-More-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He replaced the ether with occult forces based on <a href="/wiki/Hermeticism" title="Hermeticism">Hermetic</a> ideas of attraction and repulsion between particles. <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>, who acquired many of Newton\'s writings on alchemy, stated that "Newton was not the first of the age of reason: He was the last of the magicians."<sup id="cite_ref-The_Collected_Writings_of_John_Maynard_Keynes_Volume_X_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Collected_Writings_of_John_Maynard_Keynes_Volume_X-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Newton\'s interest in alchemy cannot be isolated from his contributions to science.<sup id="cite_ref-More_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-More-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This was at a time when there was no clear distinction between alchemy and science. Had he not relied on the <a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">occult</a> idea of <a href="/wiki/Action_at_a_distance_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Action at a distance (physics)">action at a distance</a>, across a vacuum, he might not have developed his theory of gravity. (See also <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton%27s_occult_studies" title="Isaac Newton&#39;s occult studies">Isaac Newton\'s occult studies</a>.)\n</p><p>In 1704, Newton published <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i>, in which he expounded his corpuscular theory of light. He considered light to be made up of extremely subtle corpuscles, that ordinary matter was made of grosser corpuscles and speculated that through a kind of alchemical transmutation "Are not gross Bodies and Light convertible into one another,&#160;... and may not Bodies receive much of their Activity from the Particles of Light which enter their Composition?"<sup id="cite_ref-Newton&#39;s_Alchemy_and_His_Theory_of_Matter_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton&#39;s_Alchemy_and_His_Theory_of_Matter-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Newton also constructed a primitive form of a frictional <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_generator" title="Electrostatic generator">electrostatic generator</a>, using a glass globe.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an article entitled "Newton, prisms, and the \'opticks\' of tunable lasers"<sup id="cite_ref-OPN1_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OPN1-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> it is indicated that Newton in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i> was the first to show a diagram using a prism as a beam expander. In the same book he describes, via diagrams, the use of multiple-prism arrays. Some 278 years after Newton\'s discussion, <a href="/wiki/Beam_expander#Multiple-prism_beam_expanders" title="Beam expander">multiple-prism beam expanders</a> became central to the development of <a href="/wiki/Laser_linewidth" title="Laser linewidth">narrow-linewidth</a> <a href="/wiki/Tunable_laser" title="Tunable laser">tunable lasers</a>. Also, the use of these prismatic beam expanders led to the <a href="/wiki/Multiple-prism_dispersion_theory" title="Multiple-prism dispersion theory">multiple-prism dispersion theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OPN1_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OPN1-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Subsequent to Newton, much has been amended. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Young</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustin-Jean_Fresnel" title="Augustin-Jean Fresnel">Fresnel</a> combined Newton\'s particle theory with <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Huygens\'</a> wave theory to show that colour is the visible manifestation of light\'s wavelength. Science also slowly came to realise the difference between perception of colour and mathematisable optics. The German poet and scientist, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>, could not shake the Newtonian foundation but "one hole Goethe did find in Newton\'s armour,&#160;... Newton had committed himself to the doctrine that refraction without colour was impossible. He therefore thought that the object-glasses of telescopes must for ever remain imperfect, achromatism and refraction being incompatible. This inference was proved by <a href="/wiki/John_Dollond" title="John Dollond">\nDollond</a> to be wrong."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_(4670220).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_%284670220%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1039" data-file-height="1269" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Sir_Isaac_Newton_(4670220).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraving of a Portrait of Sir Isaac Newton by <a href="/wiki/John_Vanderbank" title="John Vanderbank">John Vanderbank</a></div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanics_and_gravitation">Mechanics and gravitation</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Writing_of_Principia_Mathematica" title="Writing of Principia Mathematica">Writing of Principia Mathematica</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewtonsPrincipia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/NewtonsPrincipia.jpg/220px-NewtonsPrincipia.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/NewtonsPrincipia.jpg/330px-NewtonsPrincipia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/NewtonsPrincipia.jpg/440px-NewtonsPrincipia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1266" data-file-height="842" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewtonsPrincipia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newton\'s own copy of his <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i>, with hand-written corrections for the second edition</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1679, Newton returned to his work on <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">celestial mechanics</a> by considering gravitation and its effect on the orbits of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> with reference to <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Kepler&#39;s laws">Kepler\'s laws</a> of planetary motion. This followed stimulation by a brief exchange of letters in 1679\xe2\x80\x9380 with Hooke, who had been appointed to manage the Royal Society\'s correspondence, and who opened a correspondence intended to elicit contributions from Newton to Royal Society transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-hooke1679nov24_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hooke1679nov24-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Newton\'s reawakening interest in astronomical matters received further stimulus by the appearance of a comet in the winter of 1680\xe2\x80\x931681, on which he corresponded with <a href="/wiki/John_Flamsteed" title="John Flamsteed">John Flamsteed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980391\xe2\x80\x932_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980391\xe2\x80\x932-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> After the exchanges with Hooke, Newton worked out proof that the elliptical form of planetary orbits would result from a centripetal force inversely proportional to the square of the radius vector (see <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation#History" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">Newton\'s law of universal gravitation&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 History</a> and <i>De motu corporum in gyrum</i>). Newton communicated his results to <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> and to the Royal Society in <i><a href="/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum" title="De motu corporum in gyrum">De motu corporum in gyrum</a></i>, a tract written on about nine sheets which was copied into the Royal Society\'s Register Book in December 1684.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> This tract contained the nucleus that Newton developed and expanded to form the <i>Principia</i>.\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i> was published on 5 July 1687 with encouragement and financial help from <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a>. In this work, Newton stated the three universal laws of motion. Together, these laws describe the relationship between any object, the forces acting upon it and the resulting motion, laying the foundation for <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a>. They contributed to many advances during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> which soon followed and were not improved upon for more than 200 years. Many of these advancements continue to be the underpinnings of non-relativistic technologies in the modern world. He used the Latin word <i>gravitas</i> (weight) for the effect that would become known as <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, and defined the law of <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">universal gravitation</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In the same work, Newton presented a calculus-like method of geometrical analysis using \'first and last ratios\', gave the first analytical determination (based on <a href="/wiki/Boyle%27s_law" title="Boyle&#39;s law">Boyle\'s law</a>) of the speed of sound in air, inferred the oblateness of Earth\'s spheroidal figure, accounted for the precession of the equinoxes as a result of the Moon\'s gravitational attraction on the Earth\'s oblateness, initiated the gravitational study of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_theory#Newton" title="Lunar theory">irregularities in the motion of the moon</a>, provided a theory for the determination of the orbits of comets, and much more.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Newton made clear his <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> view of the Solar System\xe2\x80\x94developed in a somewhat modern way, because already in the mid-1680s he recognised the "deviation of the Sun" from the centre of gravity of the Solar System.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> For Newton, it was not precisely the centre of the Sun or any other body that could be considered at rest, but rather "the common centre of gravity of the Earth, the Sun and all the Planets is to be esteem\'d the Centre of the World", and this centre of gravity "either is at rest or moves uniformly forward in a right line" (Newton adopted the "at rest" alternative in view of common consent that the centre, wherever it was, was at rest).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newton\'s postulate of an invisible <a href="/wiki/Action_at_a_distance_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Action at a distance (physics)">force able to act over vast distances</a> led to him being criticised for introducing "<a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">occult</a> agencies" into science.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Later, in the second edition of the <i>Principia</i> (1713), Newton firmly rejected such criticisms in a concluding <a href="/wiki/General_Scholium" title="General Scholium">General Scholium</a>, writing that it was enough that the phenomena implied a gravitational attraction, as they did; but they did not so far indicate its cause, and it was both unnecessary and improper to frame hypotheses of things that were not implied by the phenomena. (Here Newton used what became his famous expression <i>"hypotheses non-fingo"</i><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>).\n</p><p>With the <i>Principia</i>, Newton became internationally recognised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980Chapter_11_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980Chapter_11-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> He acquired a circle of admirers, including the Swiss-born mathematician <a href="#Duillier">Nicolas Fatio de Duillier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hatch_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hatch-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_cubics">Classification of cubics</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cubic_plane_curve" title="Cubic plane curve">Cubic plane curve</a></div>\n<p>Newton found 72 of the 78 "species" of cubic curves and categorized them into four types.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2018)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1717, and probably with Newton\'s help, <a href="/wiki/James_Stirling_(mathematician)" title="James Stirling (mathematician)">James Stirling</a> proved that every cubic was one of these four types. Newton also claimed that the four types could be obtained by <a href="/wiki/Projective_plane" title="Projective plane">plane projection</a> from one of them, and this was proved in 1731, four years after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Later_life_of_Isaac_Newton" title="Later life of Isaac Newton">Later life of Isaac Newton</a></div>\n<p>In the 1690s, Newton wrote a number of <a href="/wiki/Religious_tracts" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious tracts">religious tracts</a> dealing with the literal and symbolic interpretation of the Bible. A manuscript Newton sent to <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> in which he disputed the fidelity of <a href="/wiki/1_John_5:7" class="mw-redirect" title="1 John 5:7">1 John 5:7</a> and its fidelity to the original manuscripts of the New Testament, remained unpublished until 1785.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even though a number of authors have claimed that the work may have indicated that Newton disputed the  <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, others assure that Newton did question the passage but never denied the Trinity as such. His biographer, scientist <a href="/wiki/Sir_David_Brewster" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir David Brewster">Sir David Brewster</a>, who compiled his manuscripts for over 20 years, wrote about the controversy in a well-known book <i>Memoirs of the Life, Writings, and Discoveries of Sir Isaac Newton</i>, where he explains that Newton questioned the veracity of those passages, but he never denied the doctrine of the Trinity as such. Brewster states that Newton was never known as an <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">Arian</a> during his lifetime, it was <a href="/wiki/William_Whiston" title="William Whiston">William Whiston</a> (an Arian) who first argued that "Sir Isaac Newton was so hearty for the Baptists, as well as for the Eusebians or Arians, that he sometimes suspected these two were the two witnesses in the Revelations," while others like <a href="/wiki/Hopton_Haynes" title="Hopton Haynes">Hopton Haynes</a> (a Mint employee and Humanitarian), "mentioned to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Baron_(dissenting_minister)" title="Richard Baron (dissenting minister)">Richard Baron</a>, that Newton held the same doctrine as himself".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Brewster\'s views are, however, severely outdated, encrypted manuscripts written by Newton have been bought by <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> (among others), then deciphered<sup id="cite_ref-The_Collected_Writings_of_John_Maynard_Keynes_Volume_X_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Collected_Writings_of_John_Maynard_Keynes_Volume_X-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> and it became known that Newton has rejected Triniarianism.<sup id="cite_ref-heretic_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heretic-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newton_25.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Newton_25.jpg/170px-Newton_25.jpg" width="170" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Newton_25.jpg/255px-Newton_25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Newton_25.jpg/340px-Newton_25.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1185" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newton_25.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isaac Newton in old age in 1712, portrait by <a href="/wiki/Sir_James_Thornhill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir James Thornhill">Sir James Thornhill</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Later works\xe2\x80\x94<i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronology_of_Ancient_Kingdoms_Amended" title="The Chronology of Ancient Kingdoms Amended">The Chronology of Ancient Kingdoms Amended</a></i> (1728) and <i>Observations Upon the Prophecies of Daniel and the Apocalypse of St. John</i> (1733)\xe2\x80\x94were published after his death. He also devoted a great deal of time to <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> (see above).\n</p><p>Newton was also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> for <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Cambridge University (UK Parliament constituency)">Cambridge University</a> in 1689\xe2\x80\x9390 and 1701\xe2\x80\x932, but according to some accounts his only comments were to complain about a cold draught in the chamber and request that the window be closed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1997232_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1997232-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>  He was however noted by Cambridge diarist <a href="/wiki/Abraham_de_la_Pryme" title="Abraham de la Pryme">Abraham de la Pryme</a> as having rebuked students who were frightening local residents by claiming that a house was haunted.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newton moved to London to take up the post of warden of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">Royal Mint</a> in 1696, a position that he had obtained through the patronage of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Montagu,_1st_Earl_of_Halifax" title="Charles Montagu, 1st Earl of Halifax">Charles Montagu, 1st Earl of Halifax</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>. He took charge of England\'s great recoining, somewhat treading on the toes of Lord Lucas, Governor of the Tower (and securing the job of deputy <a href="/wiki/Comptroller" title="Comptroller">comptroller</a> of the temporary Chester branch for Edmond Halley). Newton became perhaps the best-known <a href="/wiki/Master_of_the_Mint" title="Master of the Mint">Master of the Mint</a> upon the death of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Neale" title="Thomas Neale">Thomas Neale</a> in 1699, a position Newton held for the last 30 years of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-Mint_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mint-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> These appointments were intended as <a href="/wiki/Sinecure" title="Sinecure">sinecures</a>, but Newton took them seriously, retiring from his Cambridge duties in 1701, and exercising his power to reform the currency and punish <a href="/wiki/Debasement" title="Debasement">clippers</a> and counterfeiters.\n</p><p>As Warden, and afterwards Master, of the Royal Mint, Newton estimated that 20 percent of the coins taken in during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recoinage_of_1696" title="Great Recoinage of 1696">Great Recoinage of 1696</a> were <a href="/wiki/Counterfeit" title="Counterfeit">counterfeit</a>. Counterfeiting was <a href="/wiki/High_treason_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="High treason in the United Kingdom">high treason</a>, punishable by the felon being <a href="/wiki/Hanged,_drawn_and_quartered" title="Hanged, drawn and quartered">hanged, drawn and quartered</a>. Despite this, convicting even the most flagrant criminals could be extremely difficult. However, Newton proved equal to the task.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1997259_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1997259-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Disguised as a habitu\xc3\xa9 of bars and taverns, he gathered much of that evidence himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1997267_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1997267-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> For all the barriers placed to prosecution, and separating the branches of government, <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a> still had ancient and formidable customs of authority. Newton had himself made a <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_the_peace" title="Justice of the peace">justice of the peace</a> in all the home counties\xe2\x80\x94there is a draft of a letter regarding this matter stuck into Newton\'s personal first edition of his <i>Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica</i> which he must have been amending at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Then he conducted more than 100 cross-examinations of witnesses, informers, and suspects between June 1698 and Christmas 1699. Newton successfully prosecuted 28 coiners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall200773_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall200773-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ENG_COA_Newton.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/ENG_COA_Newton.svg/170px-ENG_COA_Newton.svg.png" width="170" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/ENG_COA_Newton.svg/255px-ENG_COA_Newton.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/ENG_COA_Newton.svg/340px-ENG_COA_Newton.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="409" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ENG_COA_Newton.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coat of arms of the Newton family of Gunnerby, Lincolnshire, afterwards used by Sir Isaac<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Coat_of_arms_of_Isaac_Newton_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coat_of_arms_of_Isaac_Newton-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>As a result of a report written by Newton on 21 September 1717 to the Lords Commissioners of His Majesty\'s Treasury the bimetallic relationship between gold coins and silver coins was changed by Royal proclamation on 22 December 1717, forbidding the exchange of gold guineas for more than 21 silver shillings.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> This inadvertently resulted in a silver shortage as silver coins were used to pay for imports, while exports were paid for in gold, effectively moving Britain from the <a href="/wiki/Silver_standard" title="Silver standard">silver standard</a> to its first <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>. It is a matter of debate as whether he intended to do this or not.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> It has been argued that Newton conceived of his work at the Mint as a continuation of his alchemical work.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newton was made President of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> in 1703 and an associate of the French <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">Acad\xc3\xa9mie des Sciences</a>. In his position at the Royal Society, Newton made an enemy of <a href="/wiki/John_Flamsteed" title="John Flamsteed">John Flamsteed</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Royal" title="Astronomer Royal">Astronomer Royal</a>, by prematurely publishing Flamsteed\'s <i>Historia Coelestis Britannica</i>, which Newton had used in his studies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite1997317_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite1997317-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 1705, Queen Anne <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">knighted</a> Newton during a royal visit to Trinity College, Cambridge. The knighthood is likely to have been motivated by political considerations connected with the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary elections">Parliamentary election</a> in May 1705, rather than any recognition of Newton\'s scientific work or services as Master of the Mint.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Newton was the second scientist to be knighted, after Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newton was one of many people who lost heavily when the <a href="/wiki/South_Sea_Company" title="South Sea Company">South Sea Company</a> collapsed.  Their most significant trade was slaves, and according to his niece, he lost around \xc2\xa320,000.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Towards the end of his life, Newton took up residence at <a href="/wiki/Cranbury_Park" title="Cranbury Park">Cranbury Park</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Winchester" title="Winchester">Winchester</a> with his niece and her husband, until his death in 1727.<sup id="cite_ref-Yonge6_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yonge6-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> His half-niece, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Barton_Conduitt" class="mw-redirect" title="Catherine Barton Conduitt">Catherine Barton Conduitt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall198044_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall198044-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> served as his hostess in social affairs at his house on <a href="/wiki/Jermyn_Street" title="Jermyn Street">Jermyn Street</a> in London; he was her "very loving Uncle",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980595_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestfall1980595-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> according to his letter to her when she was recovering from <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>\n<p>Newton died in his sleep in London on 20 March 1727 (<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">OS</a> 20 March 1726; <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">NS</a> 31 March 1727).<sup id="cite_ref-OSNS_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OSNS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> His body was buried in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> may have been present at his funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> A bachelor, he had divested much of his estate to relatives during his last years, and died <a href="/wiki/Intestacy" title="Intestacy">intestate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton,_Isaac_(1642\xe2\x80\x931727)_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton,_Isaac_(1642\xe2\x80\x931727)-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> His papers went to John Conduitt and Catherine Barton.<sup id="cite_ref-Mann_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mann-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> After his death, Newton\'s hair was examined and found to contain <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, probably resulting from his alchemical pursuits. <a href="/wiki/Mercury_poisoning" title="Mercury poisoning">Mercury poisoning</a> could explain Newton\'s eccentricity in late life.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton,_Isaac_(1642\xe2\x80\x931727)_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton,_Isaac_(1642\xe2\x80\x931727)-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_relations">Personal relations</span></h3>\n<p>Although it was claimed that he was once engaged,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Newton never married. The French writer and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, who was in London at the time of Newton\'s funeral, said that he "was never sensible to any passion, was not subject to the common frailties of mankind, nor had any commerce with women\xe2\x80\x94a circumstance which was assured me by the physician and surgeon who attended him in his last moments".<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The widespread belief that he died a <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">virgin</a> has been commented on by writers such as mathematician <a href="/wiki/Charles_Hutton" title="Charles Hutton">Charles Hutton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> economist <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and physicist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><span id="Duillier"></span>Newton had a close friendship with the Swiss mathematician <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Fatio_de_Duillier" title="Nicolas Fatio de Duillier">Nicolas Fatio de Duillier</a>, whom he met in London around 1689<sup id="cite_ref-Hatch_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hatch-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94some of their correspondence has survived.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Their relationship came to an abrupt and unexplained end in 1693, and at the same time Newton suffered a <a href="/wiki/Nervous_breakdown" class="mw-redirect" title="Nervous breakdown">nervous breakdown</a><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> which included sending wild accusatory letters to his friends <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pepys" title="Samuel Pepys">Samuel Pepys</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>\xe2\x80\x94his note to the latter included the charge that Locke "endeavoured to embroil me with woemen".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManuel1968219_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManuel1968219-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="After_death">After death</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton_in_popular_culture" title="Isaac Newton in popular culture">Isaac Newton in popular culture</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fame">Fame</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg/170px-Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg/255px-Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg/340px-Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="984" data-file-height="1352" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Isaac_Newton_grave_in_Westminster_Abbey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newton\'s tomb monument in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The mathematician <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Lagrange" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Louis
